The Slanderer and the Hadith on Children of Adultery
Ibn 'Asakir reports a story of Bilal rejecting slander based on a hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadith 'No one slanders except a child of adultery' was used by a righteous judge to reject slander and its implications for justice.

Core Idea
Slander is so grave it is compared to illegitimate birth.

This lesson presents a historical account from Ibn 'Asakir's work, where Bilal ibn Abi Burda, a judge, is approached by a slanderer. Instead of accepting the accusation, he orders an investigation and then invokes a powerful hadith from the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him). The hadith serves as a severe rebuke against slander, highlighting its spiritual danger.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation to understand the narrative flow.
Note the chain of transmission at the beginning.
2
Identify the actions of Bilal
Notice that Bilal does not immediately accept the slander. He orders an investigation first. Then, upon hearing a report, he declares 'Allahu Akbar' and cites the hadith.
3
Reflect on the hadith
The hadith states: 'No one slanders people except a child of adultery.' Think about why such a strong statement is used. What does it teach about the sin of slander?
The common misunderstanding is that it is literal; it is a severe rebuke.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأخبرنا الشيخ أبو غالب أحمد بن الحسن بن البناء أخبرنا أبو محمد الحسن بن علي الجوهري أنا أبو الفضل عبيد الله بن عبد الرحمن بن محمد الزهري نا جعفر بن أحمد بن محمد القافلاني نا إبراهيم بن الوليد الجشاش أبو إسحاق حدثني سعد بن عبد الحميد نا الحسن بن خالد البصري نا محمد بن ثابت قال: جاء رجل إلى بلال بن أبي بردة فسعى برجل فقال لصاحب شرطته سل عنه فسأل عنه فقال أصلح الله الأمير إنه ليقال فيه فقال الله أكبر حدثني أبي عن جدي أبي موسى رضي الله عنه قال: قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم (لا يسعى بالناس إلا ولد زنا).
And the shaykh Abu Ghalib Ahmad ibn al-Hasan ibn al-Banna' informed us, saying: Abu Muhammad al-Hasan ibn 'Ali al-Jawhari informed us, saying: Abu al-Fadl 'Ubayd Allah ibn 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Muhammad al-Zuhri informed us, saying: Ja'far ibn Ahmad ibn Muhammad al-Qaflanani narrated to us, saying: Ibrahim ibn al-Walid al-Jashash Abu Ishaq narrated to us, saying: Sa'd ibn 'Abd al-Hamid narrated to me, saying: al-Hasan ibn Khalid al-Basri narrated to us, saying: Muhammad ibn Thabit narrated to us, saying: A man came to Bilal ibn Abi Burda and slandered a man. He said to his police chief: Ask about him. So he asked about him and said: May Allah rectify the commander, it is said about him such-and-such. He said: Allahu Akbar! My father narrated to me from my grandfather Abu Musa, may Allah be pleased with him, who said: The Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said: 'No one slanders people except a child of adultery.'
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents a story with a full chain of transmission. A man came to Bilal ibn Abi Burda to slander (sa'a) another person. Instead of accepting the accusation, Bilal ordered his police chief to investigate. When the chief reported that the accused had a reputation, Bilal exclaimed 'Allahu Akbar' and cited a hadith from his grandfather Abu Musa al-Ash'ari directly from the Prophet: 'No one slanders people except a child of adultery (walad zina).' The hadith is used to reject the slander entirely, as the act of slandering (sa'y) is considered a grave sin, associated with illegitimate birth. This shows Bilal's reliance on prophetic tradition to uphold justice and discourage tale-bearing.

Key Terms
الساعي
ولد الزنا
أبو موسى الأشعري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the hadith means that every slanderer is physically born from adultery.
Correction: The hadith is a severe rebuke, not a literal statement about lineage. It is meant to deter people from slander by highlighting its spiritual filth.

Checkpoint
What did Bilal do when a man came to him with a slander?
A) Immediately punished the accused
B) Dismissed the accuser without hearing
C) Investigated first and then cited a hadith against slander
D) Asked for witnesses

Key Takeaway
Slander is a grave sin that the Prophet compared to illegitimate birth, and a righteous leader must investigate before accepting accusations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever spoken about someone without knowing the full truth? How does knowing that the Prophet equated such slander to being born of adultery shake you? What will you do the next time you are tempted to gossip?
?
Bilal did not act on the slander until he investigated. When was the last time you defended someone from a false accusation? How can you be more like Bilal in your community?
?
The hadith is a reminder that the one who habitually slanders is like a child of adultery. Look at your own speech: does this description fit you? Repent today and make a firm intention to guard your tongue.

Al-Ash'ari's Forty-Year Study with al-Jubba'i
Ibn 'Asakir states that al-Ash'ari was al-Jubba'i's student for 40 years.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the significance of al-Ash'ari's forty-year study under al-Jubba'i and how it shaped his later critique of Mu'tazilism.

Core Idea
Forty years inside Mu'tazilism made al-Ash'ari its most formidable critic.

This passage from Ibn 'Asakir's Tabyin Kidhb al-Muftari establishes a key biographical fact: Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari spent four decades as a student of the leading Mu'tazili scholar, Abu 'Ali al-Jubba'i. Rather than undermining his authority, this long association shows that his eventual departure from Mu'tazilism was based on deep, firsthand knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the English translation. Notice the chain of transmission (isnad) that Ibn 'Asakir uses to authenticate this report.
The word 'راما' at the beginning is likely 'وأما' meaning 'and as for'.
2
Identify the key fact
The passage explicitly states that al-Ash'ari was a student (tilmīdh) of al-Jubba'i and did not leave him for forty years. Underline this duration.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why the author emphasizes the forty-year period. It shows al-Ash'ari's deep immersion in Mu'tazili thought before his departure.
This detail is often used to argue that al-Ash'ari's later refutation of Mu'tazilism came from thorough knowledge, not ignorance.
4
Connect to the broader narrative
This passage sets the stage for al-Ash'ari's famous conversion story. Understanding his long tutelage helps appreciate the weight of his later theological shift.
The text cuts off at 'wa kana' (and he was) – likely continuing to describe his later stance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
راما ذكر فضله هو في نفسه مما شهد له به العلماء من أبناء جنسه فأخبرنا الشيخ أبو القسم بن أبي العباس المالكي أنا جدي أبو محمد بن أبي نصر المقري قال سمعت الحسن بن علي بن إبراهيم المقري يقول سمعت أبا محمد الحسن بن محمد العسكري بالأهواز وكان من المخلصين في مذهبه المتقدمين في نصرته يعني مذهب الأشعري يقول: كان الأشعري تلميذ الجبائي يدرس عليه فيتعلم منه ويأخذ عنه لا يفارقه أربعين سنة وكان
And as for mentioning his own merit, it is from what the scholars of his kind have testified to him. So the shaykh Abu al-Qasim ibn Abi al-'Abbas al-Maliki informed us, saying: My grandfather Abu Muhammad ibn Abi Nasr al-Muqri' informed us, saying: I heard al-Hasan ibn 'Ali ibn Ibrahim al-Muqri' say: I heard Abu Muhammad al-Hasan ibn Muhammad al-'Askari in al-Ahwaz - and he was among the sincere ones in his madhhab and the foremost in supporting it, meaning the Ash'ari madhhab - saying: Al-Ash'ari was a student of al-Jubba'i; he studied under him, learning from him, not leaving him for forty years. And he was...
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage introduces the personal merits of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. The author states that scholars of his own kind (i.e., Ash'aris) have testified to his virtue. He then cites a chain through al-Maliki, al-Muqri', and al-'Askari, who was a devoted supporter of the Ash'ari school. Al-'Askari reports that al-Ash'ari was a student (tilmidh) of Abu 'Ali al-Jubba'i, the leading Mu'tazili scholar of the time. He studied under him, learning from him, and did not leave him for forty years. This establishes al-Ash'ari's deep immersion in Mu'tazili thought before his departure from it. The text cuts off at 'wa kana' (and he was) at the page end, likely continuing to describe his later shift. The point is to show that al-Ash'ari's eventual critique of Mu'tazilism came from thorough knowledge, not ignorance.

Key Terms
الأشعري
الجبائي
تلميذ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ash'ari was never a Mu'tazili; he always opposed them.
Correction: The report shows he was al-Jubba'i's student for forty years, meaning he was deeply immersed in Mu'tazilism before he formulated his own school.

Checkpoint

B) Twenty years
C) Thirty years
D) Forty years

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's forty-year study under al-Jubba'i demonstrates that his critique of Mu'tazilism came from deep internal knowledge, not ignorance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Ash'ari spent 40 years learning a system from the inside before he could critique it. What belief or worldview have you spent that kind of time studying? If none, what is stopping you from diving deeper into your own convictions?
?
Are there ideas you reject without having first understood them from their own advocates, as al-Ash'ari did? Will you commit to learning one opposing viewpoint thoroughly before dismissing it?
?
The report shows that al-Ash'ari did not leave his teacher for 40 years. Who in your life has that kind of patient commitment to teaching you? How have you responded to their investment in you?

The Linguistic Precision of 'Halqa'
Ibn Asakir narrates a story about Bishr ibn Abi Burdah and a linguistic clarification on the word 'halqa'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct Arabic usage of 'halqa' and its variants, and the importance of linguistic accuracy in Islamic tradition.

Core Idea
One vowel change can alter meaning: 'halqa' (circle) vs 'halaq' (shavers/razors).

This lesson presents a story from Bishr ibn Abi Burdah, who sent a man to investigate gatherings in the mosque. The man returned reporting 'people of Arabic' sitting in circles, but his pronunciation 'halqa halqa' was corrected. This leads to a scholarly clarification on the proper word for a circle in Arabic.

Guided Path
1
Read the story of Zuraiq and Bishr
Read the narration about Zuraiq being sent to check on 'people of desires' in the mosque. Notice how Bishr corrects his language.
The word 'halqa' is used twice in the story.
2
Identify the linguistic issue
Al-Khattabi explains that 'halqa' is used for a circle of people or an earring, while 'halaq' is the plural of 'haaliq' (shaver).
This distinction is crucial for correct Arabic.
3
Reflect on the significance of linguistic precision
Why did Bishr bother to correct the word? Accurate language preserves religious texts and understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كذلك أخبرنا الشيخ أبو عبدالله محمد ابن الفضل بن احمد الفراوى أخبرنا أبو الحسن عبد الغافر بن محمد بن عبد الغافر الفارسي انا أبو سلیمان حمد بن محمد بن ابراهيم الخطابي البستى اخبرني احمد بن ابراهيم بن مالك نا الدغولي يعني ابا العباس محمد بن عبدالرحمن السرخسى نا المظفرى يعنى محمد بن حاتم نا أبو بهز بن أبي الخطاب السلمى قال : كان زريع أبو يزيد بن زريع على عسس بلال بن أبي بردة قال فقال له بلغنى ان اهل الأهواء يجتمعون في المسجد ويتناز عون فاذهب فتعرف ذلك قال فذهب ثم رجع اليه فقال ما وجدت فيه إلا أهل العربية حلقة حلقة فقال ألا جلست اليهم حتى لاتقول حلقة حلقة قال أبو سليمان الخطابي وانما هى الحلقة حلقة القوم وحلقة القرط ونحوها أخبرني أبو عمرو انا ثعلب عن عمرو بن أبي عمرو الشيباني عن ابيه قال لا اقول حلقة الا فى جمع حالق أخبرنا الشيخ أبو غالب احمد بن الحسن بن البناء أخبرنا أبو محمد الحسن بن على الجوهرى انا أبو الفضل عبيد الله بن عبدالرحمن بن محمد الزهرى نا جعفر بن احمد بن محمد القافلانى نا إبراهيم بن الوليد الجشاش أبو اسحق حدثني سعد بن عبد الحميد نا الحسن بن خالد البصرى نامحمد بن ثابت قال : جاء رجل إلى بلال بن أبي بردة فسعى برجل فقال لصاحب شرطته سل عنه فسأل عنه فقال اصلح الله الأمير انه ليقال فيه فقال الله اكبر حدثني أبي عن جدى أبى موسی رضی الله عنه قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ( لا يسعى بالناس إلا ولد زني ) . فهذا ما حضرني من مناقب أبي موسى وأولاده وفي جميع ذلك فضيلة للأمام أبي الحسن و افتخار بأجداده.
Similarly, our shaykh Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn al-Fadl ibn Ahmad al-Farawi informed us: Abu al-Hasan Abd al-Ghafir ibn Muhammad ibn Abd al-Ghafir al-Farisi informed us: Abu Sulayman Hamd ibn Muhammad ibn Ibrahim al-Khattabi al-Busti informed us: Ahmad ibn Ibrahim ibn Malik narrated to us: al-Daghuli, meaning Abu al-Abbas Muhammad ibn Abd al-Rahman al-Sarakhsi, narrated to us: al-Muzafari, meaning Muhammad ibn Hatim, narrated to us: Abu Bahz ibn Abi al-Khattab al-Sulami said: Zuraiq Abu Yazid ibn Zuraiq was on night watch for Bishr ibn Abi Burdah. He said: He said to him: It has reached me that the people of desires gather in the mosque and dispute, so go and find out about that. He said: So he went, then returned to him and said: I found nothing there except people of Arabic, circle by circle. He said: Why did you not sit with them so that you would not say 'circle, circle'? Abu Sulayman al-Khattabi said: It is only 'al-halqah': the circle of people and the ring of an earring and similar. Abu Amr informed me: Tha'lab narrated to us from Amr ibn Abi Amr al-Shaybani from his father, who said: I only say 'halaq' in the plural of 'haaliq'. Our shaykh Abu Ghalib Ahmad ibn al-Hasan ibn al-Banna informed us: Abu Muhammad al-Hasan ibn Ali al-Jawhari informed us: Abu al-Fadl Ubayd Allah ibn Abd al-Rahman ibn Muhammad al-Zuhri narrated to us: Ja'far ibn Ahmad ibn Muhammad al-Qafalani narrated to us: Ibrahim ibn al-Walid al-Jashash Abu Ishaq narrated to us: Sa'd ibn Abd al-Hamid narrated to us: al-Hasan ibn Khalid al-Basri and Muhammad ibn Thabit said: A man came to Bishr ibn Abi Burdah and slandered a man. He said to his police chief: Investigate him. He inquired about him and said: May Allah rectify the commander, he is spoken of [negatively]. He said: Allahu Akbar. My father narrated to me from my grandfather Abu Musa, may Allah be pleased with him, who said: The Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said: 'No one slanders people except a child of adultery.' So this is what came to me of the virtues of Abu Musa and his children, and in all that is a virtue for Imam Abu al-Hasan and pride in his ancestors.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a biographical narration with a hadith and linguistic clarification. It begins with a chain of transmission from various scholars, leading to a story about Zuraiq, who was on night watch for Bishr ibn Abi Burdah. Bishr heard that 'people of desires' (ahl al-ahwa) were gathering in the mosque to dispute, and he sent Zuraiq to investigate. Zuraiq returned and reported that he found only 'people of Arabic' sitting in circles (halqat). Bishr then rebuked him for using the word 'halqah' incorrectly, saying he should have sat with them so as not to make that mistake. The narrator Abu Sulayman al-Khattabi then explains the correct usage: 'halqah' is used for a circle of people or an earring, while 'halaq' is used for the plural of 'haaliq' (a razor or a person who shaves). Another chain confirms this linguistic point. Then a separate narration is given: a man slandered someone to Bishr ibn Abi Burdah, who ordered an investigation. The investigator said the accused was spoken of negatively, and Bishr exclaimed 'Allahu Akbar' and narrated a hadith from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari that the Prophet said: 'No one slanders people except a child of adultery.' The author then concludes that these are the virtues of Abu Musa and his children, and through them, a virtue for Imam Abu al-Hasan (al-Ash'ari) and pride in his ancestors.

Key Terms
أهل الأهواء
حلقة
حالق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'halqa' and 'halaq' are interchangeable for a circle of people.
Correction: Halqa is used for a circle of people or an earring; halaq is the plural of haaliq (a person who shaves) or 'razors'.

Checkpoint
What did Bishr ibn Abi Burdah ask Zuraiq to investigate?
A) People of desires gathering in the mosque
B) People of Arabic learning
C) A group of slanderers
D) A ring of earrings

Key Takeaway
Precision in Arabic is crucial; one vowel change distinguishes a circle of people from shavers, as shown in this narration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever mispronounced an Arabic word in a religious context, and what could that have risked in terms of misunderstanding? Will you commit to verifying pronunciations from now on?
?
When you learn a new Arabic term from the Quran or hadith, do you also memorize its exact diacritical marks? What is one term you will check today?
?
This story shows that even a small vowel change can lead to a completely different meaning. Where in your own Islamic learning have you been careless about details, and what will you do to correct that starting this week?

The Hadith Against Slander and the Virtue of Abu Musa
Ibn Asakir narrates a hadith condemning slander and links it to the virtues of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari and Imam al-Ash'ari.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hadith forbidding slander and its connection to the virtues of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari and his descendant Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari.

Core Idea
Slander is a grave sin; the Prophet equated it with being a child of adultery.

This lesson presents a hadith narrated by Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, reported by his grandson Bishr ibn Abi Burdah, that the Prophet said: 'No one slanders people except a child of adultery.' Ibn Asakir uses this hadith to highlight the virtue of Abu Musa and his family, ultimately linking it to the honor of Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari.

Guided Path
1
Read the story of the slander
A man came to Bishr ibn Abi Burdah and slandered another. Bishr ordered an investigation, then exclaimed 'Allahu Akbar' and narrated the hadith.
Notice the chain of transmission goes back to Abu Musa.
2
Understand the hadith
The Prophet said: 'No one slanders people except a child of adultery.' This is a strong condemnation.
The word 'يسعى' means to slander or carry tales.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ibn Asakir concludes that this hadith is part of the virtues of Abu Musa and his children, and a source of pride for Imam al-Ash'ari.
This shows how lineage and prophetic praise intertwine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كذلك أخبرنا الشيخ أبو عبدالله محمد ابن الفضل بن احمد الفراوى أخبرنا أبو الحسن عبد الغافر بن محمد بن عبد الغافر الفارسي انا أبو سلیمان حمد بن محمد بن ابراهيم الخطابي البستى اخبرني احمد بن ابراهيم بن مالك نا الدغولي يعني ابا العباس محمد بن عبدالرحمن السرخسى نا المظفرى يعنى محمد بن حاتم نا أبو بهز بن أبي الخطاب السلمى قال : كان زريع أبو يزيد بن زريع على عسس بلال بن أبي بردة قال فقال له بلغنى ان اهل الأهواء يجتمعون في المسجد ويتناز عون فاذهب فتعرف ذلك قال فذهب ثم رجع اليه فقال ما وجدت فيه إلا أهل العربية حلقة حلقة فقال ألا جلست اليهم حتى لاتقول حلقة حلقة قال أبو سليمان الخطابي وانما هى الحلقة حلقة القوم وحلقة القرط ونحوها أخبرني أبو عمرو انا ثعلب عن عمرو بن أبي عمرو الشيباني عن ابيه قال لا اقول حلقة الا فى جمع حالق أخبرنا الشيخ أبو غالب احمد بن الحسن بن البناء أخبرنا أبو محمد الحسن بن على الجوهرى انا أبو الفضل عبيد الله بن عبدالرحمن بن محمد الزهرى نا جعفر بن احمد بن محمد القافلانى نا إبراهيم بن الوليد الجشاش أبو اسحق حدثني سعد بن عبد الحميد نا الحسن بن خالد البصرى نامحمد بن ثابت قال : جاء رجل إلى بلال بن أبي بردة فسعى برجل فقال لصاحب شرطته سل عنه فسأل عنه فقال اصلح الله الأمير انه ليقال فيه فقال الله اكبر حدثني أبي عن جدى أبى موسی رضی الله عنه قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ( لا يسعى بالناس إلا ولد زني ) . فهذا ما حضرني من مناقب أبي موسى وأولاده وفي جميع ذلك فضيلة للأمام أبي الحسن و افتخار بأجداده.
Similarly, our shaykh Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn al-Fadl ibn Ahmad al-Farawi informed us: Abu al-Hasan Abd al-Ghafir ibn Muhammad ibn Abd al-Ghafir al-Farisi informed us: Abu Sulayman Hamd ibn Muhammad ibn Ibrahim al-Khattabi al-Busti informed us: Ahmad ibn Ibrahim ibn Malik narrated to us: al-Daghuli, meaning Abu al-Abbas Muhammad ibn Abd al-Rahman al-Sarakhsi, narrated to us: al-Muzafari, meaning Muhammad ibn Hatim, narrated to us: Abu Bahz ibn Abi al-Khattab al-Sulami said: Zuraiq Abu Yazid ibn Zuraiq was on night watch for Bishr ibn Abi Burdah. He said: He said to him: It has reached me that the people of desires gather in the mosque and dispute, so go and find out about that. He said: So he went, then returned to him and said: I found nothing there except people of Arabic, circle by circle. He said: Why did you not sit with them so that you would not say 'circle, circle'? Abu Sulayman al-Khattabi said: It is only 'al-halqah': the circle of people and the ring of an earring and similar. Abu Amr informed me: Tha'lab narrated to us from Amr ibn Abi Amr al-Shaybani from his father, who said: I only say 'halaq' in the plural of 'haaliq'. Our shaykh Abu Ghalib Ahmad ibn al-Hasan ibn al-Banna informed us: Abu Muhammad al-Hasan ibn Ali al-Jawhari informed us: Abu al-Fadl Ubayd Allah ibn Abd al-Rahman ibn Muhammad al-Zuhri narrated to us: Ja'far ibn Ahmad ibn Muhammad al-Qafalani narrated to us: Ibrahim ibn al-Walid al-Jashash Abu Ishaq narrated to us: Sa'd ibn Abd al-Hamid narrated to us: al-Hasan ibn Khalid al-Basri and Muhammad ibn Thabit said: A man came to Bishr ibn Abi Burdah and slandered a man. He said to his police chief: Investigate him. He inquired about him and said: May Allah rectify the commander, he is spoken of [negatively]. He said: Allahu Akbar. My father narrated to me from my grandfather Abu Musa, may Allah be pleased with him, who said: The Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said: 'No one slanders people except a child of adultery.' So this is what came to me of the virtues of Abu Musa and his children, and in all that is a virtue for Imam Abu al-Hasan and pride in his ancestors.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a biographical narration with a hadith and linguistic clarification. It begins with a chain of transmission from various scholars, leading to a story about Zuraiq, who was on night watch for Bishr ibn Abi Burdah. Bishr heard that 'people of desires' (ahl al-ahwa) were gathering in the mosque to dispute, and he sent Zuraiq to investigate. Zuraiq returned and reported that he found only 'people of Arabic' sitting in circles (halqat). Bishr then rebuked him for using the word 'halqah' incorrectly, saying he should have sat with them so as not to make that mistake. The narrator Abu Sulayman al-Khattabi then explains the correct usage: 'halqah' is used for a circle of people or an earring, while 'halaq' is used for the plural of 'haaliq' (a razor or a person who shaves). Another chain confirms this linguistic point. Then a separate narration is given: a man slandered someone to Bishr ibn Abi Burdah, who ordered an investigation. The investigator said the accused was spoken of negatively, and Bishr exclaimed 'Allahu Akbar' and narrated a hadith from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari that the Prophet said: 'No one slanders people except a child of adultery.' The author then concludes that these are the virtues of Abu Musa and his children, and through them, a virtue for Imam Abu al-Hasan (al-Ash'ari) and pride in his ancestors.

Key Terms
ولد زني
يسعى
مناقب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Slander is a minor sin.
Correction: The Prophet equated habitual slander with being a child of adultery, indicating its severity.

Checkpoint
According to the hadith narrated by Abu Musa, who does the Prophet say slanders people?
A) A person of desires
B) A child of adultery
C) A person of Arabic
D) A person who shaves

Key Takeaway
The hadith against slander serves as a virtue for Abu Musa and his family, and through them, for Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you spoke ill of someone behind their back? How does the Prophet's severe warning—equating slander to being born from adultery—make you feel about that moment? Will you seek forgiveness from Allah and the person you wronged?
?
This hadith is transmitted through a chain that ends with Imam al-Ash'ari as a source of pride. Do you take pride in the scholars of Ahl al-Sunnah, or do you neglect their teachings? What one habit will you change to honor the legacy they preserved?
?
If you knew that every slanderous word you utter is a sign of spiritual illegitimacy, would you still speak carelessly? What concrete step will you take today to guard your tongue, even in jest?

Al-Ash'ari's Journey from Student to Independent Scholar
Ibn Asakir narrates al-Ash'ari's student life and his repentance after a debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the story of Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari's relationship with his teacher al-Jubba'i and the event that led to his repentance from Mu'tazilism.

Core Idea
Intellectual honesty can lead to transformative change, even after decades of loyalty.

This passage from Ibn Asakir's Tabyin Kadhib al-Muftari presents a fascinating biographical account of Imam al-Ash'ari. It highlights his long association with his teacher al-Jubba'i and a pivotal debate that, through his own fairness, eventually led him to abandon Mu'tazilism. Let's explore this story.

Guided Path
1
Read the historical narrative
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the chain of narration and the details about al-Ash'ari's relationship with al-Jubba'i.
2
Analyze the roles of teacher and student
Compare al-Ash'ari's skills in debate with al-Jubba'i's skills in authorship. How did they complement each other?
3
Examine the key debate incident
Pay attention to al-Ash'ari's reaction when the opponent was defeated. Why did he reject the celebration?
4
Understand the significance of his repentance
Reflect on how this incident led to al-Ash'ari's public transition from Mu'tazilism. What does this tell us about his character?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأخبرنا الشيخ أبو القسم بن أبي العباس المالكي انا جدى أبو محمد بن أبي نصر المقرى قال سمعت الحسن بن على بن ابراهيم المقرى يقول سمعت ابا محمد الحسن بن محمد العسكرى بالاهواز وكان من المخلصين في مذهبه المتقدمين فى نصرته يعنى مذهب الأشعرى يقول : كان الأشعرى تلميذ الجبائي يدرس عليه فيتعلم منه ويأخذ عنه لا يفارقه اربعين سنة وكان صاحب نظر في المجالس وذا إقدام على الخصوم ولم يكن من اهل التصنيف وكان إذا اخذ القلم يكتب ربما ينقطع وربما يأتى بكلام غير مرضى وكان أبو على الجبائي صاحب تصنيف وقلم اذا صنف يأتي بكل ما اراد مستقصى واذا حضر المجالس وناظر لم يكن بمرض وكان اذا دهمه الحضور في المجالس يبعث الأشعرى ويقول له نب عنى ولم يزل على ذلك زمانا فلما كان يوما حضر الأشعرى نائبا عن الجبائي في بعض المجالس وناظره انسان فانقطع في يده وكان معه رجل من العامة فنثر عليه لوزا وسكرا فقال له الأشعرى ماصنعت شيئا خصمي استظهر على واوضح الحجة وانقطعت في هو احق بالنثار منى ثم انه بعد ذلك اظهر التوبة ( ۱ ) والانتقال عن مذهبه، هذه الحكاية تدل على قوة أبي الحسن رحمه الله في المناظرة واطراحه فيها ما يستعمله بعض المجادلين من المكابرة، وتنبىء عن وفور عقله و انصافه لاقراره بظهور خصمه واعترافه .
Our shaykh Abu al-Qasim ibn Abi al-Abbas al-Maliki informed us: my grandfather Abu Muhammad ibn Abi Nasr al-Muqri narrated to us: he said: I heard al-Hasan ibn Ali ibn Ibrahim al-Muqri say: I heard Abu Muhammad al-Hasan ibn Muhammad al-Askari in al-Ahwaz—and he was among the sincere ones in his madhhab and the foremost in supporting it, meaning the Ash'ari madhhab—say: al-Ash'ari was the student of al-Jubba'i; he studied under him, learned from him, and took from him, not leaving him for forty years. He was a man of insight in gatherings and bold against opponents, but he was not among the people of authorship. When he took the pen to write, perhaps he would break down and perhaps produce unsatisfactory speech. Abu Ali al-Jubba'i was a man of authorship and pen; when he authored, he produced everything he wanted exhaustively, but when he attended gatherings and debated, he was not satisfactory. When the pressure of attending gatherings came upon him, he would send al-Ash'ari and say to him: 'Deputize for me.' He continued like that for a time. Then one day, al-Ash'ari attended as a deputy for al-Jubba'i in some gathering, and a man debated him, and [the opponent] was defeated at his hand. There was with him a common man who scattered almonds and sugar over him. al-Ash'ari said to him: 'You have done nothing; my opponent overpowered me and made the proof clear, and I was defeated; he is more deserving of the scattering than I.' Then after that, he manifested repentance and transition from his madhhab. This story indicates the strength of Abu al-Hasan, may Allah have mercy on him, in debate and his rejection in it of what some debaters use of obstinacy, and it indicates the abundance of his intellect and his fairness due to his acknowledgment of his opponent's superiority and his admission.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account of Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, focusing on his relationship with his teacher al-Jubba'i and his eventual departure from Mu'tazilism. It begins with a chain of transmission through al-Askari, who reports that al-Ash'ari was al-Jubba'i's student for forty years, learning from him continuously. Al-Ash'ari was known for his insight and boldness in debates, but he initially lacked skill in authoring books—when he tried to write, he would often get stuck or produce unsatisfactory work. In contrast, al-Jubba'i was a skilled author but a poor debater. When al-Jubba'i was called to a debate, he would send al-Ash'ari as his deputy. On one occasion, al-Ash'ari attended as deputy and an opponent debated him; the opponent was defeated. A common man celebrated by scattering sweets, but al-Ash'ari rebuked him, acknowledging that the opponent had actually gotten the better of him and presented a clear proof. This honesty and fairness led al-Ash'ari to later publicly renounce his Mu'tazili beliefs. The author then comments that this story demonstrates al-Ash'ari's strength in debate, his rejection of obstinacy, his intellectual integrity, and his fairness in admitting defeat.

الجبائي
تلميذ
المناظرة
التوبة
الانتقال عن مذهبه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Ash'ari's repentance was a sudden, unprovoked event.
Correction: The passage shows it was preceded by a long period of study and a specific moment where his intellectual honesty compelled him to admit his opponent's superiority.

Checkpoint

D) Fifty years

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's fairness in acknowledging his opponent's superior argument led him to eventually abandon his long-held beliefs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
This story shows that even a great scholar like al-Ash'ari had to go through a process of recognition and repentance. Is there an area in your life where you need to acknowledge that you have been following falsehood or weak arguments? What step can you take today to seek the truth with honesty?

Al-Ash'ari's Authorship: Defense and Number of Works
Ibn Asakir defends al-Ash'ari's mature works and records the number of his writings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Asakir's defense of al-Ash'ari's mature writings and know the reported number of his works.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari's later works are excellent; early struggles do not define him.

Ibn Asakir tackles a criticism head-on: al-Ash'ari's early compositions were said to be poor. But he reframes this as a pre-guidance state, insisting that his mature works are commendable and refined. Then, he provides a chain of transmission giving the number of al-Ash'ari's works as fifty-five.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the flow: first a defense, then a report with a chain of narrators.
2
Identify the two main sections
The passage clearly has two parts: a defense against the criticism of poor authorship, and a factual report of the number of works via a chain.
The defense uses phrases like 'مستحسنة مهذبة' and 'مستجادة مستصوبة'.
3
Reflect on the authority of the report
Ibn Asakir cites a chain from Ibn Hazm through al-Khatib al-Baghdadi. This shows the importance of isnad even in biographical details.
Consider why Ibn Asakir includes this chain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاما ما ذكر فيها عنه من رداءة التصنيف وجمود خاطره عند الأخذ في التأليف فانما اريد بذلك حالته في الابتداء لابعد ما من الله عليه به من الاهتداء فإن تصانيفه مستحسنة مهذبة وتواليفه وعباراته مستجادة مستصوبة، وقد أخبرنا الشيخان أبو الحسن على بن احمد بن منصور الفقيه وأبو منصور محمد بن عبدالملك بن الحسن بن خيرون المقرى قال على وانا وقال محمد انا أبو بكر احمد بن علی بن ثابت الحافظ قال ذكر ابر محمد علی بن احمد بن سعيد بن حزم الاندلسی ان ابا الحسن الأشعرى له خمسة وخمسون تصنيفا، وقد ترك ابن حزم من عدد مصنفاته
As for what was mentioned in it about his poor authorship and the dullness of his mind when embarking on composition, that was intended only for his state at the beginning, not after Allah granted him guidance. Indeed, his authored works are commendable and refined, and his compositions and expressions are deemed good and correct. And the two shaykhs, Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Ahmad ibn Mansur al-Faqih and Abu Mansur Muhammad ibn Abd al-Malik ibn al-Hasan ibn Khayrun al-Muqri, informed us: Ali said 'and I', and Muhammad said 'we were informed by Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Ali ibn Thabit al-Hafiz, who said: Ibrahim Muhammad Ali ibn Ahmad ibn Sa'id ibn Hazm al-Andalusi mentioned that Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari has fifty-five works, and Ibn Hazm omitted from the number of his works.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a defense of al-Ash'ari's authoring ability and a record of the number of his works. The author addresses a criticism that al-Ash'ari's early attempts at authorship were poor and his mind was dull. He explains that this refers only to his initial state before Allah guided him, and that his later works are 'commendable and refined' (mustahsana muhadhdhaba) and 'deemed good and correct' (mustajada mustaswaba). The author then cites two shaykhs who transmitted from al-Hafiz Abu Bakr al-Khatib al-Baghdadi, who reported that Ibn Hazm mentioned that al-Ash'ari had fifty-five works. The sentence ends with 'and Ibn Hazm omitted from the number of his works', possibly meaning that Ibn Hazm's count is incomplete or that he left some out. This passage defends al-Ash'ari's scholarly output and indicates the large volume of his writings.

Al-Ash'ari's Intellectual Honesty and Repentance
Ibn Asakir narrates al-Ash'ari's early difficulty with writing and his repentance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the story of al-Ash'ari's early struggles with writing, his honesty in debate, and how that led to his repentance and departure from the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
True knowledge requires honesty, even when it means admitting defeat.

This lesson presents a famous biographical anecdote about Imam al-Ash'ari. It shows that before he became a great theologian, he struggled with writing but excelled in oral debate. His honesty in admitting defeat led to a transformative repentance. As we read Ibn Asakir's narration, we will see how this event shaped his departure from the Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Read the story carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the narrative flow. Then read the English translation. Notice the contrast between al-Ash'ari and al-Jubba'i.
Pay attention to the roles: al-Ash'ari as deputy, and his reaction to defeat.
2
Identify the key characters and their traits
Identify what each character excelled in: al-Ash'ari in debating, al-Jubba'i in writing. Understand why al-Jubba'i sent al-Ash'ari as deputy.
3
Reflect on al-Ash'ari's reaction to defeat
Notice that when al-Ash'ari lost the debate, he refused the celebratory gesture and admitted his opponent's merit. This honesty led to his repentance.
Consider what this says about his character and integrity.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صاحب نظر في المجالس وذا إقدام على الخصوم ولم يكن من اهل التصنيف وكان إذا اخذ القلم يكتب ربما ينقطع وربما يأتى بكلام غير مرضى وكان أبو على الجبائي صاحب تصنيف وقلم اذا صنف يأتي بكل ما اراد مستقصى واذا حضر المجالس وناظر لم يكن بمرض وكان اذا دهمه الحضور في المجالس يبعث الأشعرى ويقول له نب عنى ولم يزل على ذلك زمانا فلما كان يوما حضر الأشعرى نائبا عن الجبائي في بعض المجالس وناظره انسان فانقطع في يده وكان معه رجل من العامة فنثر عليه لوزا وسكرا فقال له الأشعرى ماصنعت شيئا خصمي استظهر على واوضح الحجة وانقطعت في هو احق بالنثار منى ثم انه بعد ذلك اظهر التوبة والانتقال عن مذهبه، هذه الحكاية تدل على قوة أبي الحسن رحمه الله في المناظرة واطراحه فيها ما يستعمله بعض المجادلين من المكابرة، وتنبىء عن وفور عقله و انصافه لاقراره بظهور خصمه واعترافه .
He was a man of insight in assemblies and bold against opponents, but he was not one of the people of authorship. When he took the pen to write, sometimes he would break off and sometimes he would produce unsatisfactory speech. Abu 'Ali al-Jubba'i was a man of authorship and pen; when he authored, he produced everything he wanted comprehensively, and when he attended assemblies and debated, he was not satisfactory. When the situation pressed him in assemblies, he would send al-Ash'ari and say to him: 'Deputize for me.' He continued like that for a time. Then one day al-Ash'ari attended deputizing for al-Jubba'i in some assembly, and a man debated him and was defeated by him. There was a common man with him who scattered almonds and sugar over him. Al-Ash'ari said to him: 'You have done nothing; my opponent prevailed over me and made the proof clear, and I was defeated. He is more deserving of the scattered gifts than I.' Then after that he manifested repentance and abandoning of his madhhab. This story indicates the strength of Abu al-Hasan, may Allah have mercy on him, in debate and his abandonment in it of what some disputants use of obstinacy, and it tells of his abundant intellect and fairness due to his acknowledgment of the appearance of his opponent and his confession.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a biographical anecdote about al-Ash'ari's early career. It describes how he was not skilled in writing at first, unlike al-Jubba'i, and how al-Jubba'i would send him as a deputy in debates. In one debate, al-Ash'ari actually lost to his opponent but honestly admitted his defeat, rejecting the sugar and almonds thrown at him by a commoner. This incident led to his repentance and departure from the Mu'tazilite madhhab. Ibn Asakir then comments that this story shows al-Ash'ari's strength in debate, his rejection of the common tactic of stubbornness (mukabara), and his fairness in acknowledging the truth even against himself.

Key Terms
النظر
المناظرة
المكابرة
التوبة
الخصم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ash'ari's early inability to write discredits his later scholarship.
Correction: This story actually highlights his intellectual honesty; his writing ability improved later, but his honesty was a virtue from the start.

Checkpoint
What did al-Ash'ari do when he was defeated in the debate mentioned in the story?
A) He became angry and left.
B) He accepted the sugar and almonds.
C) He admitted his opponent was more deserving of the praise.
D) He argued further with the commoner.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's honest admission of defeat in debate led to his repentance and ultimately to his formulation of Sunni theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you, like al-Ash'ari, admitted you were wrong in a religious discussion? How did that feel, and what did it lead to?
?
Al-Ash'ari refused the gifts because he recognized the truth was on the side of his opponent. Is there an area of your life where you are clinging to a falsehood because it's more comfortable or because you've invested too much in it? What would it take for you to say, 'I was defeated'?
?
The common man assumed al-Ash'ari won and showered him with praise, but al-Ash'ari saw reality. How often do you accept praise or validation that you know is undeserved? What would it mean for you to reject that false praise and seek the truth instead?

Defense of Al-Ash'ari's Authorship and Works
Ibn Asakir defends al-Ash'ari's later excellence and counts his works, with footnotes on repentance and Ibn Hazm's bias.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir defends al-Ash'ari's later scholarly excellence and the factors behind Ibn Hazm's biased criticism.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari's early weakness was overcome; his works are excellent, and his critics are unreliable.

In this passage, Ibn Asakir addresses criticism that al-Ash'ari's early works were poor. He clarifies that this refers to his state before guidance, and later his works became refined. He also cites Ibn Hazm's count of al-Ash'ari's works but notes Ibn Hazm's omissions and bias. Additionally, he discusses the necessity of public repentance for innovators and critiques Ibn Hazm's reliance on unreliable sources.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the transition from defense to counting works to the footnotes.
2
Identify the two main defenses
Find where Ibn Asakir defends al-Ash'ari's early state and where he cites Ibn Hazm's count. Note the correction that the criticism applies only to his initial period.
The defense is two-fold: (a) early vs later state, (b) count of works with omitted titles.
3
Analyze the footnotes
The passage includes two footnoted remarks: one about the rule of public repentance for innovators, and another criticizing Ibn Hazm's bias. Consider why Ibn Asakir includes these.
These footnotes seem to address broader sectarian issues related to the criticism of al-Ash'ari.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاما ما ذكر فيها عنه من رداءة التصنيف وجمود خاطره عند الأخذ في التأليف فانما اريد بذلك حالته في الابتداء لابعد ما من الله عليه به من الاهتداء فإن تصانيفه مستحسنة مهذبة وتواليفه وعباراته مستجادة مستصوبة، وقد أخبرنا الشيخان أبو الحسن على بن احمد بن منصور الفقيه وأبو منصور محمد بن عبدالملك بن الحسن بن خيرون المقرى قال على وانا وقال محمد انا أبو بكر احمد بن علی بن ثابت الحافظ قال ذكر ابر محمد علی بن احمد بن سعيد بن حزم الاندلسی (۲) ان ابا الحسن الأشعرى له خمسة وخمسون تصنيفا، وقد ترك ابن حزم من عدد مصنفاته (۱) ولم يكتف بالتوبة سرا لأن البدعى إذا تاب يجب عليه اظهار توبته ولا تجزئه التربة سرا كما هو المقرر عند أهل العلم. (۲) لابن حزم تحامل شديد على الأشعرية لاسيما على الباقلاني وابن فورك مع أنه لم يكن اطلع على كتب الاصحاب بالمغرب بل استغل ما بلغه فيهم من شياطين الحشوية الذين يختلقون في حقهم الافك والزور وزاد هو توليدا وتهويلا كما هو ديدنه وان لم يكن هو من الحشوية في الصفات بل مع المعتزلة في المعنى، وكان الباقلاني لا يعد داود الظاهرى (إمام ابن حزم ) في شيء من الفقه كما كان غيره يقول في حقه مثل ذلك في أصول الدين وهذا مما يهيج ابن حزم ويزيده مرضا إلى مرضه .
As for what was mentioned in it about him regarding poor authorship and freezing of his mind when undertaking composition, that was intended only to refer to his initial state before what Allah bestowed upon him of guidance. For his authored works are excellent and refined, and his compositions and expressions are deemed good and correct. And the two shaykhs, Abu al-Hasan 'Ali ibn Ahmad ibn Mansur al-Faqih and Abu Mansur Muhammad ibn 'Abd al-Malik ibn al-Hasan ibn Khayrun al-Muqri', informed us: 'Ali said: 'And I was told,' and Muhammad said: 'We were told by Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn 'Ali ibn Thabit al-Hafiz who said: 'Muhammad 'Ali ibn Ahmad ibn Sa'id ibn Hazm al-Andalusi mentioned that Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari has fifty-five authored works.' And Ibn Hazm omitted more than half of the number of his authored works. And he was not content with repentance in secret because the innovator, when he repents, is required to manifest his repentance, and repentance in secret does not suffice him, as is established among the people of knowledge. Ibn Hazm has severe bias against the Ash'aris, especially against al-Baqillani and Ibn Furak, even though he had not seen the books of the companions in the Maghrib, but rather exploited what reached him about them from the devils of the Hashwiyya who fabricate lies and falsehood against them, and he increased fabrication and exaggeration as is his habit, even though he is not from the Hashwiyya in attributes but rather with the Mu'tazila in meaning. And al-Baqillani did not consider Dawud al-Zahiri (Ibn Hazm's imam) as anything in fiqh, just as others used to say likewise about him in usul al-din, and this is what provokes Ibn Hazm and increases his sickness.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a defense of al-Ash'ari's later scholarly output. Ibn Asakir explains that the earlier criticism about poor authorship referred only to his initial state before guidance. He then cites a report from Ibn Hazm that al-Ash'ari had fifty-five works, but Ibn Hazm omitted many. Ibn Asakir also includes two footnotes: first, a rule that an innovator must publicly repent; second, a critique of Ibn Hazm's bias against Ash'aris, noting that Ibn Hazm relied on unreliable sources and that al-Baqillani had dismissed Dawud al-Zahiri's fiqh, which fueled Ibn Hazm's hostility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ash'ari's early poor writing discredits all his works.
Correction: Ibn Asakir clarifies that the criticism refers only to his initial state before guidance; his later works are excellent and refined.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Asakir, why did Ibn Hazm have a biased view of the Ash'aris?
A) He had studied their books thoroughly.
B) He relied on unreliable sources from the Hashwiyya.
C) He was a follower of al-Ash'ari.
D) He was unaware of al-Ash'ari's works.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir defends al-Ash'ari by distinguishing his early and later states and highlights Ibn Hazm's unreliable sources as the basis of his bias.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed someone's later excellence because you knew their past weakness? How can you seek Allah's guidance to see people as they are now, not as they were?
?
When you hear a criticism of a scholar or group, do you verify the sources or assume the critic is reliable? How does Ibn Hazm's reliance on fabricated reports challenge your own habit of accepting accusations at face value?
?
Are you quick to defend your own school or group (like Ibn Hazm defending his imam) even if it leads to bias? How can you cultivate fairness and objectivity in your heart before Allah?

Harun al-A'war: Turning Reproach to Victory
Ibn Asakir recounts the story of Harun al-A'war as an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a scholar's past error can be turned into a rhetorical advantage through the story of Harun al-A'war.

Core Idea
Past error can become a tool for refutation when handled wisely.

This lesson presents the first of two analogies from Ibn 'Asakir, showing how a former Jew who became Muslim used his past to defeat an opponent in debate. It illustrates the principle that knowledge of error can aid in defending truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the story of Harun al-A'war
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: a former Jew converts, excels in Islam, and when reproached for his past, he cleverly turns the reproach back on himself.
Pay attention to Harun's response: 'How badly I have done!' — this is a rhetorical move.
2
Identify the key rhetorical moment
Identify how Harun's opponent tried to use his past against him, and how Harun's response both acknowledged the past and still won the argument.
3
Reflect on the lesson
Think about how this story applies to the criticism of al-Ash'ari's former Mu'tazilism. Why does Ibn 'Asakir present this analogy?
The moral is that prior knowledge of error can be an asset, not a liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاستراحة من يعيره بذلك كاستراحة مناظر هرون بن موسى الأعور فيما أخبرنا الشيخ أبو منصور عبدالرحمن بن محمد بن عبد الواحد الشيباني ببغداد أنا أبو بكر أحمد بن على بن ثابت الخطيب حدثني الحسن بن محمد الخلال نا سليمان بن ايوب المعدل قال سمعت عبد الله بن سليمان ابن الأشعث قال سمعت أبي يقول كان هرون الاعور يهوديا فأسلم وحسن اسلامه وحفظ القرآن وضبطه وحفظ النحو فناظره انسان يوما في مسألة فغلبه هرون فلم يدر المغلوب ما يصنع فقال له انت كنت يهوديا فأسلمت فقال له هرون فبئس ما صنعت قال فغلبه ايضا في هذا، أخبرنا الشيخ أبو المظفر احمد بن الحسن بن محمد البسطامى بها انا جدى لأبي أبو الفضل بن علی بن احمد بن الحسين بن سهل السهلكي قال سمعت الفقيه بن عبد الله الرزجاهی رحمه الله قال سمعت الاستاذ الامام محمد ابا عمرو ابا سهل محمد بن سليمان الصعلوكي رحمه الله يقول حضرنا مع الشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعرى رضى الله عنه مجلس علوي بالبصرة فناظره المعتزلة الله جذلهم تعالى وكانوا كثيرا حتى أتى على الكل فهزمهم كلما انقطع . واحد أخذ الآخر حتى انقطعوا عن آخرهم فعدنا في المجلس الثاني فما عاد احد فقال بين يدى العلوى باغلام اكتب على الباب : فروا . (۱) بل لو لم يكن خالط هؤلاء النظار المعروفين بدقة النظر وطارحهم المسائل لما تمرن على الاجادة فى البحث ولم يظهر منه هذه البراعة فى الزام الخصوم والذب عن السنة ولبقى مثل الرواة الذين ابتعدوا عن السنة في معارضة المعتزلة فوقعوا في بدع اطم لجهلهم بطرق النظر وهذا مما لا ينكر وما هلك امرؤ عرف قدره ولم يتعد طوره.
So the ease of him who reproaches him for that is like the ease of the disputant Harun ibn Musa al-A'war, regarding what Shaykh Abu Mansur 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Muhammad ibn 'Abd al-Wahid al-Shaybani informed us in Baghdad: Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn 'Ali ibn Thabit al-Khatib told us: al-Hasan ibn Muhammad al-Khallal narrated to us: Sulayman ibn Ayyub al-Mu'addil said: I heard 'Abd Allah ibn Sulayman ibn al-Ash'ath say: I heard my father say: Harun al-A'war was a Jew, then he became Muslim and his Islam was good, and he memorized the Qur'an and mastered it, and memorized grammar. A man debated him one day about a matter, and Harun defeated him. The defeated one did not know what to do, so he said to him: 'You were a Jew and then you became Muslim.' Harun said to him: 'How badly I have done!' He said: So he defeated him also in this. And Shaykh Abu al-Muzaffar Ahmad ibn al-Hasan ibn Muhammad al-Bistami informed us: my maternal grandfather Abu al-Fadl ibn 'Ali ibn Ahmad ibn al-Husayn ibn Sahl al-Sahlaki told us: I heard al-Faqih ibn 'Abd Allah al-Razjahi, may Allah have mercy on him, say: I heard the master imam, Abu 'Amr Abu Sahl Muhammad ibn Sulayman al-Sa'luqi, may Allah have mercy on him, say: We attended with Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, may Allah be pleased with him, an 'Alawi assembly in Basra. The Mu'tazila debated him – Allah intensify their confusion! – and they were many, until he encompassed all of them and defeated them. Whenever one was silenced, another took over, until they were all silenced. Then we returned in the second assembly, and no one came back. So he said, in front of the 'Alawi: 'Boy, write on the door: They fled.' (1) Indeed, had he not associated with these dialecticians known for precision of thought and exchanged questions with them, he would not have trained in excellence of investigation, nor would this skill in compelling opponents and defending the Sunna have appeared from him, and he would have remained like the narrators who stayed away from the Sunna in opposing the Mu'tazila and fell into worse innovations due to their ignorance of the methods of inquiry. This is undeniable. No man perishes who knows his own worth and does not overstep his station.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage provides two analogies in response to critics who point out al-Ash'ari's previous Mu'tazilism. The first story is about Harun al-A'war, a former Jew who converted and then excelled in debating Jews; when his opponent tried to reproach him for his past, Harun cleverly turned the reproach back on himself, defeating the opponent. The second story is a direct account of al-Ash'ari's own debate with the Mu'tazila in Basra, where he vanquished them all, and they did not return to a second session. The footnote then explains that his former association with precise dialecticians actually trained him to be an effective defender of the Sunna, unlike ignorant narrators who opposed the Mu'tazila without skill and fell into worse innovations. The passage concludes with the moral that one who knows his worth and stays within his bounds does not perish.

Key Terms
المناظرة
الإسلام
التوبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that a scholar's past error disqualifies him from being a reliable authority.
Correction: This story shows that prior error, when acknowledged and learned from, can become an advantage in debate and does not disqualify one from defending the truth.

Checkpoint
How did Harun al-A'war respond to the opponent who reproached him for his former Judaism?
A) He admitted his past and apologized.
B) He said 'How badly I have done!' and still won the debate.
C) He ignored the comment.
D) He changed the subject.

Key Takeaway
Prior error, when handled properly, can become a rhetorical strength rather than a weakness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been ashamed of your past mistakes, fearing they disqualify you from being taken seriously in matters of faith? How does Harun's example challenge that fear? What is one area of your past you need to stop hiding from?
?
When someone brings up your past sins to discredit you, how do you normally react? What would it look like for you to respond with the same wisdom as Harun, turning reproach into a strength? Pray about one person you need to forgive for using your past against you.

Al-Ash'ari's Victory: Training Through Former Error
Ibn Asakir recounts al-Ash'ari's debate with the Mu'tazila and draws a lesson.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari's prior Mu'tazilism prepared him to defend Sunni orthodoxy, as illustrated by his debate with the Mu'tazila in Basra.

Core Idea
Familiarity with opponents' arguments makes one a stronger defender of truth.

This lesson presents the second analogy: a first-hand account of al-Ash'ari's debate with the Mu'tazila. It shows that his former association with them trained him to defeat them decisively. The passage concludes with a lesson about the importance of proper training in refuting innovation.

Guided Path
1
Read the account of al-Ash'ari's debate
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the chain of transmission and the vivid description of the debate: the Mu'tazila were many but al-Ash'ari defeated them all.
The phrase 'Allah intensify their confusion!' shows the author's stance.
2
Understand the outcome
Note that the Mu'tazila did not return for a second assembly, and al-Ash'ari had a boy write 'They fled' on the door. This emphasizes his complete victory.
3
Reflect on the footnote's lesson
Read the concluding part: without his prior training among dialecticians, al-Ash'ari would not have been so effective. It warns against opposing innovation without proper knowledge.
The moral is that one should know one's limits and not overstep.

C) They accepted Sunni Islam.
D) They complained to the 'Alawi.

Key Takeaway
Prior association with opponents, when properly utilized, can equip one to defend orthodoxy effectively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own learning, do you actively seek to understand the arguments of those who oppose Islamic teachings, or do you avoid them? How might your avoidance actually weaken your faith?
?
Al-Ash'ari did not fear engaging with the Mu'tazila, even after leaving them. What are the 'Mu'tazila' in your life—people or ideas you find challenging? Are you willing to study them deeply to better defend the truth?
?
The narrators fell into worse innovations because they stayed away. Where in your life have you stayed away from difficult theological issues, and how might that have left you vulnerable to confusion or error?

The Virtue of Recantation: Defending al-Ash'ari's Mu'tazili Past
Ibn 'Asakir defends al-Ash'ari's early Mu'tazilism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ibn 'Asakir argues that al-Ash'ari's early Mu'tazilism was a qualification that made him a more effective defender of Sunni Islam.

Core Idea
Knowing a school's flaws from the inside strengthens one's defense of the truth.

In this passage, Ibn 'Asakir responds to critics who pointed to al-Ash'ari's early adherence to Mu'tazilism as a fault. He turns the criticism on its head, arguing that it was in fact a virtue. He then illustrates his point with the story of Harun al-A'war, a Jewish convert who used his knowledge of Judaism to defeat a debater. This lesson unpacks his argument and the story.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation to get the overall sense of Ibn 'Asakir's argument and the story.
Pay attention to the shift from the argument to the illustrative story.
2
Identify the core claim
Find the sentence where Ibn 'Asakir states that one who recants a school is more knowledgeable of its defects. Underline or note the key phrase.
The phrase begins with 'لأن من رجع عن مذهب...'
3
Examine the analogy
Notice how the story of Harun al-A'war serves as an analogy. Compare the situation: Harun's former Judaism vs. al-Ash'ari's former Mu'tazilism.
The story shows that knowledge of a system from within is an advantage in debate.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this defense reframes al-Ash'ari's biography. Why might this argument be important for Sunni scholars defending al-Ash'ari?
This counters the charge that his early Mu'tazilism was a liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد عد بعض الجهلاء هذه الحكاية من مثالبه عند العقلاء من جملة مناقبه فأما ماذكر فيها من طول مقامه على مذهب المعتزلة فمما لا يفضى به رحمه الله إلى انحطاط المنزلة بل يقضى له في معرفة الأصول بعلو المرتبة ويدل عند ذوى البضائر له على سمو المنقبة لأن من رجع عن مذهب كان بعواره اخبر و علی رد شبه اهله و کشف تمويهاتهم اقدر وتبيين ما يلبسون به لمن يهتدى باستبصاره أبصر (۱) فاستراحة من يعيره بذلك كاستراحة مناظر هرون بن موسى الأعور فيما أخبرنا الشيخ أبو منصور عبدالرحمن بن محمد بن عبد الواحد الشيباني ببغداد أنا أبو بكر أحمد بن على بن ثابت الخطيب حدثني الحسن بن محمد الخلال نا سليمان بن ايوب المعدل قال سمعت عبد الله بن سليمان ابن الأشعث قال سمعت أبي يقول كان هرون الاعور يهوديا فأسلم وحسن اسلامه وحفظ القرآن وضبطه وحفظ النحو فناظره انسان يوما في مسألة فغلبه هرون فلم يدر المغلوب ما يصنع فقال له انت كنت يهوديا فأسلمت فقال له هرون فبئس ما صنعت قال فغلبه ايضا في هذا (۱) بل لو لم يكن خالط هؤلاء النظار المعروفين بدقة النظر وطارحهم المسائل لما تمرن على الاجادة فى البحث ولم يظهر منه هذه البراعة فى الزام الخصوم والذب عن السنة ولبقى مثل الرواة الذين ابتعدوا عن السنة في معارضة المعتزلة فوقعوا في بدع اطم لجهلهم بطرق النظر وهذا مما لا ينكر وما هلك امرؤ عرف قدره ولم يتعد طوره.
Some ignorant people have counted this story among his faults in the eyes of the intelligent, whereas it is among his virtues. As for what is mentioned in it of his long adherence to the school of the Mu'tazila, that does not cause him—may God have mercy on him—to descend in rank; rather, it grants him high station in knowledge of the principles, and indicates to those of insight his lofty merit. Because one who recants from a school is more knowledgeable of its defects, more capable of refuting the doubts of its people and uncovering their deceptions, and more perceptive in clarifying what they confuse for those who seek guidance through his insight. So the relief of one who reproaches him for that is like the relief of the debater of Harun ibn Musa al-A'war, according to what informed us... [chain] ...He said: I heard my father say: Harun al-A'war was a Jew and embraced Islam, and his Islam was good, and he memorized the Quran and mastered it, and learned grammar. A man debated him one day on a matter and Harun defeated him, and the defeated one did not know what to do, so he said to him: 'You were a Jew and then you became Muslim.' Harun said to him: 'How badly you have done!' He said: He defeated him again in this. (1) Rather, if he had not mixed with these theologians known for precision of reasoning and engaged them in discussions, he would not have trained in excellence of research, nor would this skill in confuting opponents and defending the Sunna have appeared from him; he would have remained like the narrators who stayed away from the Sunna in opposing the Mu'tazila and fell into innovations due to their ignorance of the methods of reasoning. This is not deniable, and no one who knows his worth and does not exceed his limit has perished.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a defense of Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari against critics who point to his early adherence to Mu'tazilism as a fault. Ibn 'Asakir argues that it is actually a virtue: one who leaves a school knows its flaws better, is more able to refute its arguments, and can more clearly expose its deceptions to seekers of truth. He illustrates this point with the story of Harun ibn Musa al-A'war, a Jewish convert to Islam who, because of his prior knowledge, could defeat a debater who tried to use his former religion against him. A marginal note (footnote 1) reinforces the argument: had al-Ash'ari not studied with the Mu'tazila, he would not have developed his expertise in debate and would have remained weak like those who ignorantly fell into innovation. The author then presents chains of transmission for the Harun story.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
أبو الحسن الأشعري
هرون بن موسى الأعور
المناقب
المثالب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Ash'ari's early Mu'tazilism is a flaw that discredits him as a Sunni theologian.
Correction: Ibn 'Asakir argues that it is a virtue because it gives him intimate knowledge of Mu'tazili arguments and enhances his ability to refute them.

Checkpoint

C) Because he never truly left Mu'tazilism
D) Because it was a minor sin that he repented from

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's early Mu'tazilism equipped him with the knowledge and skills to effectively refute Mu'tazili arguments and defend Sunni orthodoxy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Think of a time you were criticized for a past mistake or affiliation. Did that experience actually give you a deeper understanding that others lack? Are there areas in your life where you are avoiding the work of correcting falsehood because you feel unqualified? What would Ibn ‘Asakir say to you?
?
The passage argues that mixing with opponents trains one to defend the truth. What 'mixing' are you avoiding? Are you staying in a comfort zone of only studying what you already agree with? What step can you take today to engage with a differing viewpoint in order to strengthen your own conviction?

Al-Ash'ari's Debate in Basra and the Mu'tazilites' Flight
Narrative of al-Ash'ari defeating the Mu'tazila in a debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Ash'ari decisively defeated the Mu'tazila in a public debate in Basra, leading to their complete retreat.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari's single debate routed the entire Mu'tazili camp.

This lesson recounts a famous incident from the life of Imam al-Ash'ari, demonstrating his mastery in theological argument. The story is transmitted through a reliable chain of authorities and highlights how al-Ash'ari, despite being outnumbered, overcame every opponent until none dared to face him again. As you read the passage, notice the dramatic conclusion where he orders the word 'Flee' to be written on the door.

Guided Path
1
Read the narrative carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the full story. Note the chain of transmission and the key figures mentioned.
The chain shows the story's reliability in the Islamic scholarly tradition.
2
Visualize the debate scene
Imagine the gathering in Basra with many Mu'tazila opponents. See how al-Ash'ari dealt with them one by one until all were silenced.
The phrase 'whenever one was cut off, another took over' shows their coordinated attempt.
3
Reflect on the outcome
Note that when a second session was called, no one returned. Al-Ash'ari's victory was so complete that his opponents fled permanently.
The word 'Flee' (فروا) on the door symbolizes their utter defeat.
4
Connect to al-Ash'ari's legacy
This event underscores al-Ash'ari's role as a defender of Sunni orthodoxy and his intellectual superiority over the Mu'tazila.
It also illustrates the historical context of theological debates in early Islam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخبرنا الشيخ أبو المظفر احمد بن الحسن بن محمد البسطامى بها انا جدى لأبي أبو الفضل بن علی بن احمد بن الحسين بن سهل السهلكي قال سمعت الفقيه بن عبد الله الرزجاهی رحمه الله قال سمعت الاستاذ الامام محمد ابا عمرو ابا سهل محمد بن سليمان الصعلوكي رحمه الله يقول حضرنا مع الشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعرى رضى الله عنه مجلس علوي بالبصرة فناظره المعتزلة الله جذلهم تعالى وكانوا كثيرا حتى أتى على الكل فهزمهم كلما انقطع . واحد أخذ الآخر حتى انقطعوا عن آخرهم فعدنا في المجلس الثاني فما عاد احد فقال بين يدى العلوى باغلام اكتب على الباب : فروا . محمد
Informed us Sheikh Abu al-Muzaffar Ahmad ibn al-Hasan ibn Muhammad al-Bistami, from my maternal grandfather Abu al-Fadl ibn Ali ibn Ahmad ibn al-Husayn ibn Sahl al-Sahlaki, he said: I heard the faqih ibn Abdullah al-Razjahi, may God have mercy on him, say: I heard the professor imam Abu Amr Abu Sahl Muhammad ibn Sulayman al-Sa'luqi, may God have mercy on him, say: We attended with Sheikh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, may God be pleased with him, a gathering of Alawi in Basra. The Mu'tazila debated him—may God thwart them—and they were many, until he overcame them all and defeated them. Whenever one was cut off, another took over until they were all cut off. So we returned to the second gathering, and no one came back. Then he said before the Alawi to a boy: Write on the door: 'They fled.' Muhammad.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a biographical narrative transmitted through a chain of authorities, recounting a debate between Imam al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazila in Basra. The story emphasizes al-Ash'ari's extraordinary debating skill: despite the Mu'tazila being numerous, he defeated each one sequentially until all were silenced. When a second session was held, none of them returned, and al-Ash'ari ordered a boy to write 'Flee' (فروا) on the door, indicating their complete rout. The narrative underscores al-Ash'ari's triumph over his opponents.

Correction: The story shows they were completely overwhelmed: al-Ash'ari defeated every single opponent, and when a second session was called, none dared to return.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Ash'ari's decisive victory in the Basra debate caused the Mu'tazila to flee, cementing his reputation as a champion of Sunni theology.

Al-Ash'ari Restrains the Mu'tazila: A Lesson in Divine Support
Ibn al-Sayrafi's statement about al-Ash'ari restraining the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the role of Imam al-Ash'ari in countering the Mu'tazila as described by Ibn al-Sayrafi, and appreciate the authenticity of the narration.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari contained the Mu'tazila like sesame seeds in their pods.

This lesson presents a vivid historical account from the early Ash'ari tradition. A great scholar, Ibn al-Sayrafi, uses a striking metaphor to describe how Allah subdued the Mu'tazila through Imam al-Ash'ari. We will examine the narration's chain and its meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the meaning.
Notice the chain of transmission—it's a typical hadith-style narration.
2
Understand the metaphor
Reflect on the metaphor 'restrained them in the pods of sesame.' What does it convey about al-Ash'ari's effect on the Mu'tazila?
Sesame pods are small and contain many seeds; they are fragile but prevent scattering.
3
Assess chain authenticity
The author calls the chain 'bright like the sun.' Consider why the chain's integrity matters for this historical claim.
The narrators are all well-known and reliable.
4
Reflect on historical context
Think about the Mu'tazila's prominence before al-Ash'ari. How does this narration fit into the broader story of Ash'ari theology?
This anecdote highlights divine support for al-Ash'ari.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخبرنا الشيخ أبو الحسن علی بن احمد المالكي قال نا والشيخ أبو محمد عبدالملك بن عبد الملك الشافعي قال انا احمد بن على بن ثابت الحافظ حدثنی محمد ابن على الصورى قال سمعت عبدالغني بن سعيد الحافظ يقول سمعت ابا الحسن علی بن محمد بن يزيد يقول سمعت أبا بكر بن الصيرفى يقول كانت المعتزلة قد رفعوا رؤسهم حتى أظهر الله تعالى الأشعرى فحجزهم في اقماع السمسم، إسناد هذه الحكاية مضيء كالشمس ورواتها لا يخالج فى عدالتهم شك فى النفس وقائلها أبو بكر إمام كبير محلّه عند أهل العلم محل خطير وقد أخبرنا الشريف أبو القسم علی بن ابراهيم بن العباس الحسينى والشيخ أبو الحسن علی بن أحمد بن قبيس قالا قال لنا أبو بكر أحمد ابن علی بن ثابت الحافظ نا محمد بن عبد الله أبو بكر الفقيه الشافعى المعروف بالصيرفى له تصانيف في اصول الحديث من احمد بن منصور ومن بعده الفقه وكان فهما عالما وسمع لم يرو كبير شيء
Informed us Sheikh Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Ahmad al-Maliki, he said: and Sheikh Abu Muhammad Abd al-Malik ibn Abd al-Malik al-Shafi'i, he said: informed us Ahmad ibn Ali ibn Thabit al-Hafiz, narrated to me Muhammad ibn Ali al-Suri, he said: I heard Abd al-Ghani ibn Sa'id al-Hafiz say: I heard Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Muhammad ibn Yazid say: I heard Abu Bakr ibn al-Sayrafi say: The Mu'tazila had raised their heads until Allah—exalted is He—manifested al-Ash'ari, and He restrained them in the pods of sesame. The chain of this story is bright like the sun, and no doubt about the integrity of its narrators enters the soul. Its narrator is Abu Bakr, a great imam, whose rank among the people of knowledge is weighty. And informed us the noble Abu al-Qasim Ali ibn Ibrahim ibn al-Abbas al-Husayni and Sheikh Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Ahmad ibn Qubays, they both said: narrated to us Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Ali ibn Thabit al-Hafiz, narrated to us Muhammad ibn Abd Allah Abu Bakr al-Faqih al-Shafi'i, known as al-Sayrafi, who authored works on the principles of hadith, jurisprudence from Ahmad ibn Mansur and after, and he was insightful, knowledgeable, and heard [hadith] but did not narrate much of substance.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents a concise statement by Abu Bakr ibn al-Sayrafi, transmitted through a chain of reliable narrators, praising the effectiveness of Imam al-Ash'ari against the Mu'tazila. The metaphor 'restrained them in the pods of sesame' suggests that al-Ash'ari confined the Mu'tazila and rendered them insignificant, like sesame seeds trapped in their pods. The author then emphasizes the authenticity of the chain, calling it 'bright like the sun' and affirming the integrity of the narrators. He further provides additional biographical information about Abu Bakr al-Sayrafi, noting his scholarly stature and his works on hadith methodology.

Key Terms

أبو بكر بن الصيرفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the metaphor 'pods of sesame' implies humiliation or defeat of the Mu'tazila.
Correction: The metaphor actually emphasizes restraint and containment, not humiliation. It suggests al-Ash'ari confined the Mu'tazila, limiting their spread.

Key Takeaway

Ibn Khafif's Encounter with Al-Ash'ari's Debate Genius
Ibn Khafif describes witnessing al-Ash'ari's debate with Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Ash'ari's skill in debate was witnessed by a contemporary scholar, Ibn Khafif, and why this story is significant for his legacy.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari's ability to refute multiple opponents simultaneously astounded even fellow scholars.

This lesson presents a first-hand account from Abu Abd Allah ibn Khafif, a renowned Sufi and scholar, who traveled to Basra specifically to meet Imam al-Ash'ari. He describes observing al-Ash'ari in a debate with a group of Mu'tazila, where al-Ash'ari responded to each one individually after they had spoken. Ibn Khafif's astonished reaction reveals the extraordinary intellectual capacity of al-Ash'ari. As you read the passage, notice the chain of transmission and the vivid details that bring the scene to life.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation to understand the narrative. Notice the multiple chains of transmission.
2

Ibn Khafif is a well-known Sufi; his testimony carries weight.
3

Consider why Ibn Khafif asked about multiple tongues, ears, and eyes. What does this reveal about al-Ash'ari's debating method?
Ibn Khafif's question shows astonishment, not mockery.

Informed us Sheikh Abu al-Muzaffar Ahmad ibn al-Hasan al-Shi'ri, informed us Abu al-Fadl Muhammad ibn Ali ibn Ahmad al-Bistami, he said: I heard the judge Abu Bakr al-Husayn al-Askafi, he said: I heard the judge Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Tayyib ibn Muhammad al-Ash'ari, may God have mercy on him, say: I heard Abu Abd Allah ibn Khafif say: I entered Basra seeking Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, may God have mercy on him, and I was guided to him. He was in one of the gatherings of debate. I entered and there was a group of Mu'tazila, and they were speaking. When they fell silent and finished their speech, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari said to them one by one: 'You said such-and-such, and the answer to it is such-and-such,' until he answered all. When he stood up, I followed him and began to look him over. He said: 'What are you looking at?' I said: 'How many tongues do you have, how many ears do you have, and how many eyes do you have?' He laughed and said to me: 'Where are you from?' I said: 'From Shiraz.' And I kept company with him after that. And informed us Sheikh Abu Abd Allah Muhammad ibn al-Fadl ibn Ahmad al-Faqih al-Farawi, informed us the professor Abu al-Qasim Abd al-Karim ibn Shu'ayb al-Qushayri, he said: I heard Sheikh Abu Abd Allah Muhammad ibn Abd Allah ibn Ubayd Allah al-Shirazi al-Sufi say: I heard some companions of Abu Ubayd Allah say: I heard Abu Abd Allah ibn Khafif say: I entered Basra in my youth to see Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari when news of him reached me, and I saw an old man of noble appearance, and I said to him...

Commentary
This passage contains two versions of a story about the visit of Abu Abd Allah ibn Khafif to Imam al-Ash'ari. In the first version, Ibn Khafif seeks out al-Ash'ari in Basra and observes a debate in which al-Ash'ari, after the Mu'tazila speak, refutes each one individually, answering all their points. Amazed, Ibn Khafif asks al-Ash'ari how many tongues, ears, and eyes he has, implying that he seems superhuman. Al-Ash'ari laughs and learns he is from Shiraz; Ibn Khafif then becomes his companion. The second version, through a different chain, begins the same story but cuts off as Ibn Khafif says he saw a noble-looking old man (al-Ash'ari) and started to speak to him. The story illustrates al-Ash'ari's formidable debating skill and the wonder of those who witnessed it.

أبو الحسن الأشعري
المعتزلة
بصرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ibn Khafif was mocking al-Ash'ari when he asked about multiple organs.
Correction: Ibn Khafif's questions actually express his astonishment and admiration for al-Ash'ari's ability to track and refute multiple opponents simultaneously.

Checkpoint

A) 'Are you a prophet?'

C) 'Why did you leave the Mu'tazila?'
D) 'Will you teach me?'

Key Takeaway
Ibn Khafif's astonishment at al-Ash'ari's debate skill shows the depth of his knowledge and the respect he commanded among scholars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter someone with exceptional knowledge or skill, does your heart feel awe that drives you to seek their company and learn from them, or does it stir envy or indifference? What does your reaction reveal about your love for truth?
?
Ibn Khafif asked al-Ash'ari, 'How many tongues, ears, and eyes do you have?' — amazed at his ability to listen and respond to many at once. Do you truly listen to those who oppose your beliefs, or do you rush to answer without understanding? Will you commit to listening fully before responding, even in difficult conversations?
?
Al-Ash'ari laughed and asked where Ibn Khafif was from, and then Ibn Khafif became his companion. When someone approaches you with sincere questions, do you welcome them with warmth and openness, or do you dismiss them? Are you willing to invest in those who seek guidance, as al-Ash'ari did?
